		<category><![CDATA[Intermediate (200)]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">c836ee240d36782193404233ace1a7bde8cae97b</guid>

					<description>In this post, you build and connect that server end to end. You will implement MCP tools, set up two-layer JSON Web Token (JWT) authentication, deploy with AWS Cloud Development Kit (AWS CDK), and connect the result to Mistral AI’s Vibe. The post also covers prerequisites, solution architecture, best practices for MCP servers and Vibe connectors, and resource cleanup. The ecommerce server that you build supports product search, order placement, review submission, and returns processing using Amazon DynamoDB for data and Amazon Cognito for identity management.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;When ecommerce teams need faster time-to-market for AI-powered customer experiences, they face weeks of custom integration work that delays launches and increases security risks. Building and connecting a production-ready AI assistant typically requires custom API code for each client, container infrastructure management, and complex authentication. Amazon Bedrock AgentCore and Mistral AI Studio streamline this process. A production-ready ecommerce Model Context Protocol (MCP) server on Amazon Bedrock AgentCore, connected to Mistral AI Studio, streamlines development. The MCP provides standardized integration protocols, AgentCore Runtime manages containers and validates tokens, and Amazon Cognito handles identity.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, you build and connect that server end to end. You will implement MCP tools, set up two-layer JSON Web Token (JWT) authentication, deploy with AWS Cloud Development Kit (AWS CDK), and connect the result to &lt;a href="https://mistral.ai/products/vibe/"&gt;Mistral AI’s Vibe&lt;/a&gt;. The post also covers prerequisites, solution architecture, best practices for MCP servers and Vibe connectors, and resource cleanup. The ecommerce server that you build supports product search, order placement, review submission, and returns processing using Amazon DynamoDB for data and Amazon Cognito for identity management.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore is a platform to build, connect, and optimize AI agents at scale. Within it, AgentCore Runtime is the fully managed serverless component for hosting agent and MCP workloads, with session isolation, long-running request support, built-in JWT validation, and observability, so you don’t manage containers, load balancers, or auth middleware. In this post, you build the MCP server with Python and FastMCP, then deploy it to Runtime for managed hosting. Amazon Cognito manages user identity through OAuth 2.1, keeping each customer’s data isolated. With MCP, you write one server that multiple AI clients connect to, rather than building a separate integration for each client. Mistral AI’s Vibe gives users a conversational interface to the server on web, iOS, and Android.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;By the end, you will have a working ecommerce MCP server that authenticates users through Amazon Cognito, scopes data access per customer, and responds to natural language queries from Vibe. Because the server uses the MCP standard, other MCP-compatible clients can also connect to it.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To see the solution in action, watch the following demo. Then, explore the full post for a detailed guide on implementing your own production-ready MCP server and querying it from Vibe.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 640px;" class="wp-video"&gt;
 &lt;video class="wp-video-shortcode" id="video-134056-1" width="640" height="360" preload="metadata" controls="controls"&gt;
  &lt;source type="video/mp4" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/artifacts/DBSBlogs/ml-18668/Demo+MCP+Vibe.mp4?_=1"&gt;
 &lt;/video&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You need an &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/account/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS account&lt;/a&gt; with permissions to create &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/dynamodb/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon DynamoDB&lt;/a&gt; tables (NoSQL database), &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cognito/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Cognito&lt;/a&gt; user pools (user identity, OAuth 2.1), AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) roles, and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/ecr/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Elastic Container Registry (Amazon ECR)&lt;/a&gt; repositories, and to access &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/agentcore/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore&lt;/a&gt; (platform for building and running AI agents). For local development tools, install Python 3.10 or later, Node.js 18 or later, AWS CDK (&lt;code&gt;npm install -g aws-cdk&lt;/code&gt;), the AWS Command Line Interface (AWS CLI) configured with credentials, and the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/runtime-get-started-cli.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore CLI&lt;/a&gt; (&lt;code&gt;pip install bedrock-agentcore&lt;/code&gt;). You also need a Mistral AI account with access to Vibe. Docker isn’t required because AgentCore Runtime builds container images in the cloud using AWS CodeBuild.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-overview"&gt;Solution overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We show you how to build an ecommerce MCP server that performs real shopping operations: searching products, placing orders, submitting reviews, and processing returns. You work with three layers:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Application layer:&lt;/strong&gt; The MCP server is a Python application built with &lt;a href="https://gofastmcp.com/getting-started/welcome" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;FastMCP&lt;/a&gt;, a framework for building MCP servers. It exposes six ecommerce tools through an &lt;code&gt;/mcp&lt;/code&gt; endpoint and a &lt;code&gt;/health&lt;/code&gt; endpoint for monitoring. AgentCore Runtime runs the server as a container.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Data layer:&lt;/strong&gt; Five Amazon DynamoDB tables store ecommerce data: Products, Customers, Orders, Reviews, and Returns. You provision the tables with on-demand capacity for automatic scaling. Global Secondary Indexes support efficient query patterns.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Security layer:&lt;/strong&gt; Two-tier authentication that keeps each customer’s data private. Amazon Cognito serves as the identity provider, AgentCore Runtime validates JWT tokens at the infrastructure level, and the application extracts user-specific attributes to scope data access to the authenticated customer.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You deploy the solution using AWS CDK with four infrastructure stacks. The DynamoDBStack creates the five data tables with indexes and configures them for straightforward teardown in development environments. The CognitoStack provisions the user pool with custom attributes for customer identification and creates OAuth 2.1 app clients configured for Mistral AI Vibe integration. The DataLoaderStack uses an AWS Lambda custom resource to seed the database with realistic test data (50 products, 10 customers, 50 orders, reviews, and returns), so you can test the server immediately after deployment. The AgentCoreRuntimeStack creates an IAM role with the required permissions, provisions an ECR repository for the container image, and stores configuration parameters that the deployment command references.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img loading="lazy" class="alignnone wp-image-134547 size-full" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/Screenshot-2026-06-29-at-10.35.15 PM.png" alt="" width="2454" height="834"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Figure 1. Request flow architecture for the ecommerce MCP server&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The diagram illustrates the request flow when a user interacts with the ecommerce MCP server through Mistral Vibe. Before the first request, the user authenticates through an OAuth 2.1 login flow: Vibe opens a Cognito-hosted login page, the user enters their credentials, and Cognito issues a JWT token that Vibe stores for the session. When a user asks, “Show me my recent orders,” the AI model determines it needs to call the &lt;code&gt;get_order_history&lt;/code&gt; MCP tool and sends an MCP request over HTTPS to the AgentCore endpoint. The request includes the Bearer JWT token from the OAuth 2.1 login flow. Before reaching the application code, AgentCore’s JWT Validator verifies the token with Cognito User Pool. It checks the signature, expiration, and client authorization, then rejects invalid tokens. Once validated, the request reaches the MCP Server container, which calls Cognito to retrieve the customer ID attribute that links the authenticated user to their ecommerce profile. With the customer identity confirmed, the server queries the relevant DynamoDB tables. The operation is scoped to only that customer’s data to enforce privacy and isolation. The server then sends the structured response back through AgentCore to Vibe, which generates a natural language answer like “You have 2 recent orders: Order #1234 for Wireless Headphones (delivered) and Order #1236 for Laptop Stand (processing).”&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This architecture uses layered security. AgentCore Runtime authenticates requests at the infrastructure layer by validating the JWT signature and expiration. The application then uses the authenticated identity to scope data access to that specific customer’s orders, reviews, and returns, so one user can’t access another user’s data. The serverless design scales based on demand, and stateless containers distribute across availability zones.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="technical-walkthrough"&gt;Technical walkthrough&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this section, we walk through the key components of the ecommerce MCP server, from project structure and tool definitions to authentication and deployment on Amazon Bedrock AgentCore.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="project-organization"&gt;Project organization&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can find the complete source code and detailed implementation at this &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/mistral-on-aws/tree/main/MCP/Ecommerce_MCP_Server" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;GitHub repo.&lt;/a&gt; Three main components work together to form the MCP server:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The MCP server application lives in the &lt;code&gt;mcp_server/&lt;/code&gt; directory and contains the core business logic. &lt;code&gt;server.py&lt;/code&gt; defines the six ecommerce tools using FastMCP decorators and configures the HTTP transport. &lt;code&gt;utils/auth.py&lt;/code&gt; handles customer identity extraction from JWT tokens. &lt;code&gt;utils/dynamodb_client.py&lt;/code&gt; provides a clean interface to the five DynamoDB tables, handling common operations like product search, order creation, and review queries.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The infrastructure code in &lt;code&gt;ecommerce-mcp-cdk/&lt;/code&gt; defines the AWS resources using CDK stacks. The DynamoDB stack creates five tables with appropriate indexes for efficient queries. The Cognito stack provisions the user pool with custom attributes and two OAuth clients, one for general API access and one specifically configured for Mistral AI Studio. The &lt;code&gt;DataLoader&lt;/code&gt; stack seeds the database with realistic test data on first deployment. The &lt;code&gt;AgentCoreRuntime&lt;/code&gt; stack creates the IAM execution role with appropriate permissions for DynamoDB, Cognito, and Amazon CloudWatch, provisions the ECR repository for container images, and stores configuration values in SSM Parameter Store for reference during deployment.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The deployment configuration is captured in &lt;code&gt;.bedrock_agentcore.yaml&lt;/code&gt;, which is generated by the &lt;code&gt;agentcore configure&lt;/code&gt; command. This file tells AgentCore where to find the Cognito user pool for JWT validation, which client IDs are authorized to connect, and which request headers should be forwarded to the application.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="defining-mcp-tools"&gt;Defining MCP tools&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You define MCP tools as Python functions decorated with &lt;code&gt;@mcp.tool()&lt;/code&gt;. The function’s parameters, type hints, and docstring become the tool schema. The AI model reads this schema to decide when and how to call each tool.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Here’s an example showing how the order history tool is defined with authentication:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;@mcp.tool()
def get_order_history(limit: int = 10) -&amp;gt; dict:
    """
    Get order history for the authenticated user.

    REQUIRES AUTHENTICATION - Pass Authorization header.

    Args:
        limit: Maximum number of orders to return

    Returns:
        List of past orders with status, product details, and pricing
    """
    customer_id = get_current_customer_id()
    if customer_id == 'anonymous':
        return {"success": False, "error": "Authentication required"}

    try:
        orders = db.get_customer_orders(customer_id, limit=limit)
        # Enrich orders with product names
        enriched_orders = []
        for order in orders:
            product_id = order.get('product_id')
            if product_id:
                product = db.get_product(product_id)
                if product:
                    order['product_name'] = product.get('name', 'Unknown Product')
                    order['product_category'] = product.get('category', 'Unknown')
            enriched_orders.append(order)
        return {"success": True, "order_count": len(enriched_orders), "orders": enriched_orders}
    except Exception as e:
        return {"success": False, "error": str(e)}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This tool demonstrates several important patterns. The docstring clearly states “REQUIRES AUTHENTICATION” so the AI model understands this tool needs a logged-in user. The first lines check the customer identity and immediately return an error if the user is anonymous. This is defense in depth even though AgentCore Runtime has already validated the JWT at the infrastructure layer. The response enriches order data by joining with product information, returning both machine-readable IDs and human-friendly labels so the AI model can generate natural language responses without making additional calls.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You configure the server for stateless operation, which AgentCore requires for load balancing:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;mcp = FastMCP("ecommerce-mcp-server")
mcp_app = mcp.http_app(path="/mcp", stateless_http=True)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="two-layer-authentication"&gt;Two-layer authentication&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You split authentication across two layers, each with a specific responsibility. AgentCore Runtime owns cryptographic validation. The application code only needs to resolve the validated token into a customer identity.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;At the infrastructure layer, AgentCore Runtime validates every JWT before it reaches the application. It verifies the cryptographic signature against Cognito’s public keys and checks the issuer, expiry, and client ID against the allowed list. Invalid tokens are rejected immediately. The application code doesn’t run for unauthenticated requests.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;At the application layer, the server resolves the validated JWT into a customer identity. Since OAuth 2.1 tokens don’t include custom attributes in their payload, the server calls Cognito to retrieve the &lt;code&gt;custom:customer_id&lt;/code&gt; that links the user to their ecommerce data. It uses a dual-method approach to handle different token types:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;def extract_customer_id_from_token(access_token: str) -&amp;gt; Optional[str]:
    """
    Extract custom:customer_id from a Cognito access token.

    Handles OAuth 2.1 tokens using AdminGetUser via IAM.
    """
    cognito = boto3.client('cognito-idp', region_name=AWS_REGION)

    # Primary method: AdminGetUser for OAuth 2.1 Authorization Code tokens
    try:
        payload = _decode_jwt_payload(access_token)
        username = payload.get('username') or payload.get('sub')
        user_pool_id = payload.get('iss', '').rstrip('/').split('/')[-1]

        if username and user_pool_id:
            user_info = cognito.admin_get_user(
                UserPoolId=user_pool_id,
                Username=username
            )
            for attr in user_info['UserAttributes']:
                if attr['Name'] == 'custom:customer_id':
                    return attr['Value']
    except (ClientError, Exception):
        pass

    # Fallback method: get_user() for token types with admin scope
    try:
        user_info = cognito.get_user(AccessToken=access_token)
        for attr in user_info['UserAttributes']:
            if attr['Name'] == 'custom:customer_id':
                return attr['Value']
    except ClientError:
        pass

    return None&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The primary method decodes the JWT payload to extract the username and user pool ID, then calls &lt;code&gt;admin_get_user()&lt;/code&gt; using IAM permissions. This approach handles OAuth 2.1 tokens from Mistral Le Chat. The fallback method calls &lt;code&gt;get_user()&lt;/code&gt; directly when tokens include admin scope. After it’s retrieved, the customer ID is stored in request context and used by authenticated tools to scope database operations to that user’s data.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Note: AgentCore Identity supports custom claims in JWT tokens, which can forward attributes like &lt;code&gt;customer_id&lt;/code&gt; directly to your application without an additional API call. For more information, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/runtime-oauth.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Configuring OAuth for AgentCore Runtime&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/inbound-jwt-authorizer.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Inbound JWT Authorizer&lt;/a&gt;. This post uses the explicit &lt;code&gt;admin_get_user()&lt;/code&gt; approach instead, because it works with OpenID Connect (OIDC)-compatible identity providers and shows the full authentication flow step by step.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="deployment-workflow"&gt;Deployment workflow&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Deploying the MCP server involves four steps: infrastructure provisioning, user creation, AgentCore configuration, and container deployment.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Infrastructure provisioning&lt;/strong&gt; uses AWS CDK to create the required resources. Running &lt;code&gt;cdk deploy --all&lt;/code&gt; from the &lt;code&gt;ecommerce-mcp-cdk&lt;/code&gt; directory deploys four stacks in sequence. The deployment takes about 5 minutes and outputs the values needed for the next steps. These include the IAM role ARN, ECR repository URI, Cognito discovery URL, and client IDs. These values are also stored in SSM Parameter Store for quick retrieval.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;User creation&lt;/strong&gt; seeds the Cognito user pool with demo accounts. The &lt;code&gt;create_cognito_users.py&lt;/code&gt; script creates ten test users (demo1@example.com through demo10@example.com) and assigns each a unique customer ID that links them to their orders and reviews in DynamoDB.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;AgentCore configuration&lt;/strong&gt; tells the runtime how to validate tokens and forward requests. The &lt;code&gt;agentcore configure&lt;/code&gt; command creates the &lt;code&gt;.bedrock_agentcore.yaml&lt;/code&gt; file with the necessary settings:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;agentcore configure \
  -e server.py \
  -p MCP \
  -n ecommerce_mcp_server \
  -er $ROLE_ARN \
  -ecr $ECR_URI \
  -ac '{"customJWTAuthorizer":{"discoveryUrl":"$DISC_URL","allowedClients":["$CLIENT_ID","$MISTRAL_CLIENT_ID"]}}' \
  -rha "Authorization" \
  -r us-west-2 \
  --non-interactive&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The key configuration elements in the generated YAML file are:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-yaml"&gt;authorizer_configuration:
  customJWTAuthorizer:
    discoveryUrl: https://cognito-idp.us-west-2.amazonaws.com/us-west-2_xxxxx/.well-known/openid-configuration
    allowedClients:
      - xxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxx  # mcp-client
      - yyyyyyyyyyyyyyyyyyyyyyyyyy  # mistral-oauth-client
request_header_configuration:
  requestHeaderAllowlist:
    - Authorization&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;code&gt;discoveryUrl&lt;/code&gt; points to Cognito’s OIDC configuration endpoint, where AgentCore fetches the public keys for JWT verification. The &lt;code&gt;allowedClients&lt;/code&gt; list restricts access to authorized OAuth clients. The &lt;code&gt;requestHeaderAllowlist&lt;/code&gt; tells AgentCore to forward the Authorization header to the application. Without this setting, requests would appear anonymous.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Container deployment uses the &lt;code&gt;agentcore deploy&lt;/code&gt; command, which orchestrates a cloud-based build and deployment. AgentCore creates a CodeBuild project in your AWS account, uploads your source code to Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3), and builds an ARM64 Docker image in CodeBuild. You don’t need Docker installed locally. AgentCore then pushes the image to ECR and calls the Bedrock AgentCore API to create and start the runtime. The first invocation has a cold start of 10 to 20 seconds while the container initializes. Subsequent requests within the session respond in milliseconds.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="the-complete-flow-diagram"&gt;The complete flow diagram&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;blockquote&gt;
 &lt;p&gt;The diagram demonstrates how the solution works across four distinct phases.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/blockquote&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-18668-3.png" alt="Four-phase diagram of the solution: one-time Setup with cdk deploy and agentcore deploy; per-session Connect that establishes OAuth 2.1; per-session Discovery via list_tools; and per-request flow from Le Chat through AgentCore JWT validation, the MCP server, Cognito, and DynamoDB" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Figure 2. Four-phase architecture showing one-time setup, OAuth 2.1 connection flow, and per-request authentication&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;During the setup phase, a developer runs cdk deploy to create the AWS resources. These include the Cognito User Pool, OAuth app clients, five DynamoDB tables, IAM roles, and SSM parameters. The developer then configures AgentCore Runtime with the Cognito user pool and authorized client IDs, and runs agentcore deploy to build and deploy the MCP server container.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Connect phase happens once per user session. The user opens Vibe, adds a custom MCP connector with OAuth 2.1, and enters the AgentCore server URL and OAuth credentials. When they click Connect, Mistral discovers the Cognito identity provider and opens a browser popup for login. After the user authenticates, Cognito issues a JWT Bearer token that Mistral stores and automatically refreshes throughout the session.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Discovery phase also happens once per session, immediately after authentication. Mistral sends a &lt;code&gt;list_tools()&lt;/code&gt; request to AgentCore with the Bearer token. AgentCore validates the JWT and forwards the request to the MCP Server, which returns the six available tools and their parameter schemas. Mistral now knows which operations the server supports and what arguments each tool accepts.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Request phase occurs with every interaction. When a user asks “What electronics are in stock under $500?”, Mistral sends an MCP request to AgentCore with the JWT token. AgentCore validates the token with Cognito by checking signature, expiration, and client authorization. Once validated, the request reaches the MCP Server. The server extracts the customer identity from Cognito, queries DynamoDB for the requested data scoped to that customer, and returns formatted results. Mistral then generates a natural language response for the user.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This architecture separates deployment from runtime authentication, validates tokens at the infrastructure layer, discovers available tools before the first request, and scopes data access to the authenticated customer.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="best-practices-for-mcp-implementation"&gt;Best practices for MCP implementation&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following guidelines cover building and securing your MCP server on Amazon Bedrock AgentCore, and connecting it safely to Mistral Vibe.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="building-mcp-servers-with-agentcore"&gt;Building MCP servers with AgentCore&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When building MCP servers for deployment on Amazon Bedrock AgentCore, focus on clear tool design, layered security, and production-ready operations.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="write-tool-descriptions-that-help-ai-models-call-the-right-function"&gt;Write tool descriptions that help AI models call the right function&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;AI models rely heavily on tool descriptions when deciding which function to call. Clear, explicit documentation in your tool’s docstring directly affects how accurately the model selects and invokes tools.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Limit tool count per server: Keep each server focused with 5–8 well-defined tools rather than dozens of overlapping functions. Each additional tool increases the model’s decision complexity. If you need more operations, split them across multiple MCP servers grouped by domain (for example, one server for order management and another for product catalog). AI clients like Vibe can connect to multiple servers in a single session.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Explicit parameter guidance: Include examples in docstrings (“for example, ‘laptop’, ‘wireless headphones’”) and call out common mistakes (“Do NOT pass ‘in stock’ as query text, use the in_stock_only parameter”).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Return structured responses: Include both machine-readable identifiers (order_id, product_id) and human-readable labels (product_name, status) so the model can generate natural responses without follow-up calls.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="implement-layered-security"&gt;Implement layered security&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;AgentCore Runtime validates JWTs at the infrastructure layer, but application-level checks are equally important for defense in depth.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Validate at the tool level: Check user identity at the start of every protected function, even though AgentCore Runtime blocks unauthenticated requests at the edge. Return clear error messages for anonymous users.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Verify data ownership: Before mutations, confirm the resource belongs to the authenticated user (for example, verify &lt;code&gt;order.customer_id&lt;/code&gt; matches the token’s &lt;code&gt;customer_id&lt;/code&gt;) to prevent unauthorized access.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Apply least privilege IAM: Scope the AgentCore Runtime execution role to specific actions on specific resources. Grant only GetItem, PutItem, Query on named table ARNs, with no wildcard permissions.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Enforce tool-call boundaries with AgentCore Policy: Use AgentCore Policy to intercept tool calls before they execute. Define rules that validate which tools can be called, verify that parameters fall within acceptable ranges (for example, limit quantity to 1-100 on order placement), and reject calls that fall outside defined boundaries.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Use AgentCore Gateway for API management: Place AgentCore Gateway in front of your runtime to manage rate limiting, request routing, and additional access controls at the API layer.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Descope tokens before passing to tools: When forwarding a JWT to a tool, strip it to only the claims the tool needs. Don’t pass the full token with all scopes and attributes. This follows the principle of least privilege for token propagation and limits exposure if a tool is compromised.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="build-for-production-operations"&gt;Build for production operations&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;AgentCore Runtime manages container orchestration, but your server code should support reliable deployment and troubleshooting.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Configure stateless mode: Set &lt;code&gt;stateless_http=True&lt;/code&gt; in &lt;code&gt;mcp.http_app()&lt;/code&gt; so AgentCore can distribute requests across container instances without session affinity.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Deploy infrastructure as code: Use CDK or Terraform to make your entire stack reproducible. Include &lt;code&gt;RemovalPolicy&lt;/code&gt; settings appropriate for dev vs production environments.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Seed with realistic test data: Include a data loader that populates tables with representative records so you can validate tool behavior immediately after deployment.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="connecting-mcp-servers-to-mistral-le-chat"&gt;Connecting MCP servers to Mistral Vibe&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When connecting MCP servers to Mistral Vibe, verify that each server is trusted, properly authenticated, and scoped to only the permissions it needs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="only-add-trusted-mcp-servers"&gt;Only add trusted MCP servers&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We highly recommend connecting only to your own MCP servers or to trusted, well-documented servers, especially for tools that can run code or access sensitive systems.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Like for any web service, MCP server security starts with security basics. A good server should:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Require strong authentication (OAuth, tokens that do expire).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Enforce clear authorization rules.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Use secure connections and session handling to prevent hijacking.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before giving a connector access to your sensitive data, make sure the MCP server passes this baseline check.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;There are several ways a malicious MCP server can trick you into causing trouble:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Prompt injection: Hidden commands or instructions embedded in tool descriptions or metadata can trick the model into performing unintended actions.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Tool shadowing and typo squatting: Malicious or lookalike tools with similar names can silently override legitimate ones, leading to unexpected tool call and unintended behavior.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Over-exposure: Granting more access or functionality than needed increases your threat surface for no benefit.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Privilege escalation through token forwarding: Passing the user’s full JWT to a tool gives that tool the user’s complete privileges. A compromised tool could access resources beyond its scope. Pass only the minimum claims each tool needs.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Even a seemingly harmless tool can become a security risk if it’s deceptive and has been given enough access to your workflows. Of course, this concern doesn’t apply in the case of the ecommerce MCP server we are building ourselves.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Note that if you’re part of an enterprise Vibe plan, we only allow administrators of the plan to add custom connectors to avoid the types of security issues documented in the preceding section.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="control-the-number-of-mcp-connectors-enabled"&gt;Control the number of MCP connectors enabled&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Adding Connectors in Vibe expands what your Mistral models can do, but it also introduces trade-offs. Each new Connector adds complexity that the model must manage. Some Connectors expose dozens of functions. With only 10 Connectors active, the model might have to choose among more than 150 possible functions before deciding which one to call.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;More connectors can lead to a higher chance of error, as each additional integration increases the likelihood of misconfigured parameters, wrong tool or function call, or unexpected behaviors. Whenever feasible, we recommend limiting to 5–6 connectors active at a time.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="craft-clean-prompts"&gt;Craft clean prompts&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Even with the right Connectors, the quality of the output depends heavily on the quality of the input.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Ambiguous prompts lead to poor performance. If your request is vague, the large language model (LLM) might misinterpret the task or provide irrelevant results.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To write better prompts in Vibe:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Name the tool and action explicitly: 
  &lt;ul&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;&lt;em&gt;“Find the Q2 report”&lt;/em&gt; &lt;i data-stringify-type="italic"&gt;–&amp;gt;&lt;/i&gt; &lt;em&gt;“Use Notion to search for Q2 Revenue Report”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
  &lt;/ul&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Specify parameters: 
  &lt;ul&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;&lt;em&gt;“Show me John’s emails” ????&lt;/em&gt; “&lt;em&gt;List Gmail emails from John Smith in the last 7 days”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
  &lt;/ul&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Define scope/format: 
  &lt;ul&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;&lt;em&gt;“Check what’s planned on Monday” ???? “Check Calendar and return only event titles for Monday in a bullet list”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
  &lt;/ul&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="clean-up-resources"&gt;Clean up resources&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To avoid ongoing charges, remove the resources created in this walkthrough. First, stop the AgentCore Runtime by running &lt;code&gt;agentcore delete --name ecommerce_mcp_server&lt;/code&gt;. Then tear down the CDK stacks by running &lt;code&gt;cdk destroy --all&lt;/code&gt; from the &lt;code&gt;ecommerce-mcp-cdk&lt;/code&gt; directory. This deletes the Amazon DynamoDB tables, Amazon Cognito user pool, IAM roles, Amazon ECR repository, and related resources. The DynamoDB tables use &lt;code&gt;RemovalPolicy.DESTROY&lt;/code&gt;, so they’re deleted automatically. Verify that the resources have been removed by checking the AWS CloudFormation console.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This blog post walked through building a production-ready ecommerce MCP server using Amazon Bedrock AgentCore, Amazon DynamoDB, and Amazon Cognito, then connecting it to Mistral AI’s Vibe. We covered how to define MCP tools with clear AI-friendly documentation, implement two-layer authentication where AgentCore validates JWT tokens at the infrastructure level while the application enforces data ownership rules, deploy the solution using CDK infrastructure as code, and establish best practices for both server development and client integration. The complete source code, deployment scripts, and step-by-step guide are available in this &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/mistral-on-aws/tree/main/MCP/Ecommerce_MCP_Server" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Github repository&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The patterns in this solution apply to other domains. If you’re new to MCP, clone the repository and deploy the ecommerce server as-is. Experiment with the tools in Vibe to see how MCP request and response cycles work. Then modify a single tool to return data from your own system. If you already run workloads on AgentCore Runtime, replace the ecommerce tools with your domain-specific operations, such as a customer support tool that queries a ticket database or a financial services tool that retrieves transaction records. If you’re preparing for production, add Amazon CloudWatch dashboards for request latency and error rates, integrate AWS WAF for additional request filtering, and use Amazon EventBridge to trigger notifications on order events.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To explore related solutions, check out &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/ai/agentic-ai/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS for Autonomous Agents&lt;/a&gt; for broader agent architecture patterns, the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/agentcore-get-started-toolkit.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore documentation&lt;/a&gt; for advanced features like memory persistence and policy enforcement, and &lt;a href="https://docs.mistral.ai/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Mistral AI’s integration guides&lt;/a&gt; for connecting additional enterprise tools to Vibe. For the AWS services used in this post, see the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/amazondynamodb/latest/developerguide/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon DynamoDB Developer Guide&lt;/a&gt;, the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/cognito/latest/developerguide/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Cognito Developer Guide&lt;/a&gt;, the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/cdk/v2/guide/home.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS CDK Developer Guide&lt;/a&gt;, the &lt;a href="https://gofastmcp.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;FastMCP documentation&lt;/a&gt;, and the &lt;a href="https://modelcontextprotocol.io/specification/2025-11-25" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Model Context Protocol specification.&lt;/a&gt; We’d love to hear how you’re using MCP and AgentCore Runtime in your applications. Share your experiences in the comments or reach out to the AWS AI services team.&lt;/p&gt;

		<item>
		<title>Securing Amazon Bedrock AgentCore Runtime with AWS WAF</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/securing-amazon-bedrock-agentcore-runtime-with-aws-waf/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Puneeth Komaragiri]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 08 Jul 2026 15:57:33 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock AgentCore]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon VPC]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[AWS WAF]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Elastic Load Balancing]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">5ce366a809e1f3d4fe49ef8801be363db8f3bb0f</guid>

					<description>This post shows you two architecture patterns that address this problem. Both use an internet-facing ALB with AWS WAF and route traffic through a VPC Interface Endpoint to AgentCore Runtime. Pattern 1 places an AWS Lambda proxy between the ALB and the VPC Endpoint, giving you full control over request transformation. Pattern 2 targets the VPC Endpoint ENI IP addresses directly from the ALB, removing the Lambda hop entirely. You also learn how to close the direct-access backdoor with a resource policy so that traffic flows through AWS WAF only. Both patterns have been tested end-to-end with SigV4 and OAuth (Amazon Cognito JWT) authentication.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;When you deploy generative AI agents with &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/agentcore/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore&lt;/a&gt; as production API endpoints, you might want to enforce web application firewall policies, rate limiting, protection against common web threats, or audit controls via &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/waf/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS WAF&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;AWS WAF integrates with Elastic Load Balancing &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/elasticloadbalancing/application-load-balancer/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Application Load Balancers&lt;/a&gt; (ALBs), &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cloudfront/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon CloudFront&lt;/a&gt; distributions, and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/api-gateway/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon API Gateway&lt;/a&gt; REST APIs. Amazon CloudFront is designed for caching and content delivery. Since agent invocations are real-time and dynamic, caching doesn’t apply. Amazon API Gateway adds its own authentication and request transformation layer, which can create a double-authentication problem with the built-in SigV4 and OAuth handling in AgentCore. That leaves an internet-facing ALB as the integration point: It passes headers through transparently, supports VPC-internal routing, and attaches directly to an AWS WAF WebACL. From there, you route traffic to AgentCore through a VPC Interface Endpoint for the Bedrock AgentCore data plane service.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This is where the challenge appears. ALBs require health checks to verify that backend targets are responsive. But AgentCore Runtime requires authentication, SigV4 or OAuth, on API calls, including health check requests. Standard ALB health checks send unauthenticated requests, so they fail out of the box. You need a way to make health checks work without credentials while still passing authenticated production traffic through to AgentCore.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This post shows you two architecture patterns that address this problem. Both use an internet-facing ALB with AWS WAF and route traffic through a VPC Interface Endpoint to AgentCore Runtime. Pattern 1 places an &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/lambda/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Lambda&lt;/a&gt; proxy between the ALB and the VPC Endpoint, giving you full control over request transformation. Pattern 2 targets the VPC Endpoint ENI IP addresses directly from the ALB, removing the Lambda hop entirely. You also learn how to close the direct-access backdoor with a resource policy so that traffic flows through AWS WAF only. Both patterns have been tested end-to-end with SigV4 and OAuth (Amazon Cognito JWT) authentication.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="architecture-overview"&gt;Architecture overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The two patterns share a common foundation. A client application sends an authenticated request, either a SigV4 signature or an OAuth Bearer token, to an internet-facing ALB. AWS WAF inspects the request before the ALB forwards it to VPC Endpoint ENIs on HTTPS port 443. AgentCore validates the authentication and routes the request to the runtime container on its internal port 8080. The difference between the patterns is what sits between the ALB and the VPC Endpoint.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div id="attachment_134631" style="width: 4038px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img aria-describedby="caption-attachment-134631" loading="lazy" class="size-full wp-image-134631" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/fig1-waf.png" alt="High-level architecture, Client → AWS WAF → ALB → VPC Endpoint (PrivateLink) → AgentCore Runtime container" width="4028" height="1306"&gt;
 &lt;p id="caption-attachment-134631" class="wp-caption-text"&gt;High-level architecture, Client → AWS WAF → ALB → VPC Endpoint (PrivateLink) → AgentCore Runtime container&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The architecture has four components:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;AWS WAF&lt;/strong&gt; attaches to the ALB and provides rate limiting, SQL injection protection, XSS filtering, and bot control through managed rule groups like AWSManagedRulesCommonRuleSet.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;ALB&lt;/strong&gt; is internet-facing with an HTTPS listener on port 443, routing traffic to VPC Endpoint ENI IP targets.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/vpc/latest/privatelink/create-interface-endpoint.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;VPC Interface Endpoint&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/strong&gt; (&lt;code&gt;com.amazonaws.&amp;lt;region&amp;gt;.bedrock-agentcore&lt;/code&gt;) provides private connectivity to AgentCore through PrivateLink.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;AgentCore Runtime&lt;/strong&gt; is the managed container running your agent code, listening on internal port 8080 and supporting both SigV4 and OAuth authentication.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; Use &lt;code&gt;com.amazonaws.&amp;lt;region&amp;gt;.bedrock-agentcore&lt;/code&gt; (data plane) for Runtime invocations. There are three separate endpoint services: bedrock-agentcore (data plane for Runtime, Memory, Tools), bedrock-agentcore-control (control plane), and bedrock-agentcore.gateway (Gateway only). Using the wrong endpoint will not route to your Runtime.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To follow along with this walkthrough, you need:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AWS account with Amazon Bedrock AgentCore enabled.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AgentCore Runtime deployed (public or VPC mode).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A VPC with public and private subnets across at least two Availability Zones.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AWS Certificate Manager (ACM) certificate in the same Region, for the ALB’s HTTPS listener.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cognito/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Cognito&lt;/a&gt; User Pool (for the OAuth pattern) or AWS credentials (for the SigV4 pattern).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Familiarity with ALB, VPC Endpoints, and AWS WAF.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following sections describe two patterns for integrating AWS WAF with AgentCore. If you need request transformation, custom logging, or authentication translation between the ALB and AgentCore, start with Pattern 1. If you want the simplest architecture with the lowest latency and no additional components, skip to Pattern 2.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="pattern-1-alb-with-lambda-proxy"&gt;Pattern 1: ALB with Lambda proxy&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this pattern, a Lambda function sits behind the ALB and forwards requests, including the authentication header, to AgentCore through the VPC Endpoint. The Lambda function gives you a compute layer where you can transform requests, translate between authentication methods, or add custom logging before the request reaches AgentCore.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div id="attachment_134635" style="width: 4222px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img aria-describedby="caption-attachment-134635" loading="lazy" class="size-full wp-image-134635" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/fig2-waf.png" alt="" width="4212" height="1489"&gt;
 &lt;p id="caption-attachment-134635" class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Pattern 1 architecture, Client (JWT) → AWS WAF → ALB → Lambda Proxy → VPC → VPC Endpoint → AgentCore Runtime (OAuth authorizer)&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Choose Pattern 1 if you need:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Request transformation, header manipulation, payload changes, or protocol translation.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Custom logging or audit trails at the proxy layer.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Translation between multiple authentication methods.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An explicit compute layer between the ALB and backend (some security policies require this).&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="create-the-vpc-endpoint"&gt;Create the VPC Endpoint&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Create a VPC Interface Endpoint for the AgentCore data plane service in your private subnets:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws ec2 create-vpc-endpoint \
  --vpc-id vpc-xxxxxxxxx \
  --service-name com.amazonaws.us-east-1.bedrock-agentcore \
  --vpc-endpoint-type Interface \
  --subnet-ids subnet-aaaa subnet-bbbb \
  --security-group-ids sg-xxxxxxxxx&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Note: VPC Interface Endpoints incur hourly charges per Availability Zone plus data processing charges per GB. See &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/vpc/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;VPC pricing&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Configure the VPC Endpoint security group to allow inbound HTTPS (port 443) only from the ALB’s security group. This restricts access so that traffic routed through the ALB can reach the VPC Endpoint. For example:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws ec2 authorize-security-group-ingress \
  --group-id &amp;lt;vpce-sg-id&amp;gt; \
  --protocol tcp \
  --port 443 \
  --source-group &amp;lt;alb-sg-id&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="create-the-lambda-proxy"&gt;Create the Lambda proxy&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Lambda function receives requests from the ALB and forwards them to AgentCore through the VPC Endpoint. For OAuth, it passes the Authorization header (the Bearer token) through unchanged, along with the original request path and body, so the JWT reaches AgentCore as-is. For SigV4, the signature is bound to the original request host and becomes invalid after the request is forwarded to the VPC Endpoint, so the proxy re-signs the request using its own execution-role credentials.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following Python snippet shows the core forwarding logic. The function reads the Authorization header from the ALB event, constructs the target URL using the VPC Endpoint DNS name, and forwards the request with the original headers and body intact. This forwarding path works as-is for OAuth Bearer tokens. SigV4 requests require the additional re-signing step, which is included in the accompanying repository.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;import json
import urllib3
import os

http = urllib3.PoolManager()
VPCE_DNS = os.environ["VPCE_DNS_NAME"]

def handler(event, context):
    path = event.get("path", "/")
    headers = event.get("headers", {})
    body = event.get("body", "")
    method = event.get("httpMethod", "POST")

    target_url = f"https://{VPCE_DNS}{path}"

    resp = http.request(
        method,
        target_url,
        headers={
            "Authorization": headers.get("authorization", ""),
            "Content-Type": headers.get("content-type", "application/json"),
        },
        body=body,
    )

    return {
        "statusCode": resp.status,
        "headers": {"Content-Type": "application/json"},
        "body": resp.data.decode("utf-8"),
    }&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For brevity, the preceding snippet focuses on the forwarding logic. The complete production-ready implementation, including SigV4 signing, query string handling, ALB health check responses, and structured error handling, is available in the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-bedrock-agentcore-waf-patterns/blob/main/lambda/sigv4_proxy.py" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;accompanying GitHub repository&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Note: The Lambda proxy incurs charges per request and for compute duration (GB-seconds). See &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/lambda/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Lambda pricing&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="configure-alb-with-lambda-target-group"&gt;Configure ALB with Lambda target group&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Create a Lambda target group, register the proxy function, and create an HTTPS listener on the ALB. The ALB handles TLS termination and forwards the decrypted request to the Lambda function.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Note: Application Load Balancers incur hourly charges plus Load Balancer Capacity Unit (LCU) charges based on traffic. See &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/elasticloadbalancing/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Elastic Load Balancing pricing&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="attach-aws-waf"&gt;Attach AWS WAF&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Associate an AWS WAF WebACL with the ALB. A typical starting configuration includes AWSManagedRulesCommonRuleSet for protection against common web threats, a rate-based rule to throttle excessive requests per source IP, and AWSManagedRulesBotControlRuleSet if your agents are exposed to browser-based clients. You can add or remove managed rule groups based on your threat model. These are examples, not a prescriptive minimum.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Note: AWS WAF incurs charges per WebACL per month, per rule, and per million requests inspected. See &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/waf/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS WAF pricing&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; Lambda target groups don’t use path-based health checks. The ALB verifies the function is invocable by calling it directly, so no special health check configuration is needed.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="pattern-1-trade-offs"&gt;Pattern 1 trade-offs&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;table style="width: 693px;height: 239px" border="1" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Aspect&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Impact&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Latency&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Adds 50–200 ms per request (Lambda cold start). Mitigate with provisioned concurrency.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Cost&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Lambda invocation cost per request.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Complexity&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Additional component to deploy, monitor, and maintain.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Flexibility&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Full control over request/response transformation and custom logging.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; The Lambda proxy adds approximately 50–200 ms of latency per request. For latency-sensitive workloads, consider Pattern 2, which removes this overhead.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="pattern-2-alb-directly-to-vpc-endpoint"&gt;Pattern 2: ALB directly to VPC Endpoint&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this pattern, the ALB targets the VPC Endpoint ENI IP addresses directly on HTTPS port 443. There is no Lambda function and no Network Load Balancer. The ALB passes authentication headers through to AgentCore transparently. This architecture has fewer components and removes the Lambda proxy hop.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div id="attachment_134634" style="width: 4157px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img aria-describedby="caption-attachment-134634" loading="lazy" class="size-full wp-image-134634" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/fig3-waf.png" alt="" width="4147" height="1494"&gt;
 &lt;p id="caption-attachment-134634" class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Pattern 2 architecture, Client (JWT or SigV4) → AWS WAF → ALB → VPC Endpoint ENIs (HTTPS 443) → AgentCore Runtime&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Choose Pattern 2 if you want:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A simpler architecture with fewer components.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;No request transformation between the ALB and AgentCore.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Minimal latency with no additional hops.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Your client application to handle SigV4 or OAuth signing directly.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="create-the-vpc-endpoint-1"&gt;Create the VPC Endpoint&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Create a VPC Interface Endpoint for com.amazonaws.&amp;lt;region&amp;gt;.bedrock-agentcore in your private subnets, using the same command shown in Pattern 1, Step 1.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="retrieve-the-vpc-endpoint-eni-ip-addresses"&gt;Retrieve the VPC Endpoint ENI IP addresses&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Get the private IP addresses of the ENIs created by the VPC Endpoint. These IPs become the targets for your ALB target group.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;# Get the ENI IDs
aws ec2 describe-vpc-endpoints \
  --vpc-endpoint-ids vpce-xxxxxxxxx \
  --query "VpcEndpoints[0].NetworkInterfaceIds"

# Get the private IPs
aws ec2 describe-network-interfaces \
  --network-interface-ids eni-aaaa eni-bbbb \
  --query "NetworkInterfaces[].PrivateIpAddress"&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="create-an-ip-target-group-with-a-health-check"&gt;Create an IP target group with a health check&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This step is the key configuration that makes Pattern 2 work. Create an IP-type target group on HTTPS port 443 with a health check that doesn’t require authentication.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws elbv2 create-target-group \
  --name agentcore-vpce-tg \
  --target-type ip \
  --protocol HTTPS \
  --port 443 \
  --vpc-id vpc-xxxxxxxxx \
  --health-check-protocol HTTPS \
  --health-check-port 443 \
  --health-check-path / \
  --matcher HttpCode=200-499&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Note: This target group is available at no additional cost, but the associated Application Load Balancer incurs hourly plus LCU charges, and the VPC Interface Endpoint incurs hourly and data processing charges. See &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/elasticloadbalancing/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Elastic Load Balancing pricing&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The health check uses path / with a matcher of 200–499. AgentCore returns a 404 on / because it isn’t a valid API path, but the ALB accepts responses in the 200–499 range as healthy. This works because the goal is to verify that the VPC Endpoint is responding. The specific status code doesn’t matter. This approach requires no authentication for health checks.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="register-the-targets"&gt;Register the targets&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Register the VPC Endpoint ENI IPs as targets:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws elbv2 register-targets \
  --target-group-arn &amp;lt;tg-arn&amp;gt; \
  --targets Id=10.0.1.100,Port=443 Id=10.0.2.200,Port=443&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="create-the-https-listener"&gt;Create the HTTPS listener&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Create an HTTPS listener on the ALB that forwards to the target group. The ALB handles TLS termination using your ACM certificate:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws elbv2 create-listener \
  --load-balancer-arn &amp;lt;alb-arn&amp;gt; \
  --protocol HTTPS \
  --port 443 \
  --certificates CertificateArn=&amp;lt;acm-cert-arn&amp;gt; \
  --default-actions Type=forward,TargetGroupArn=&amp;lt;tg-arn&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="attach-aws-waf-1"&gt;Attach AWS WAF&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Associate an AWS WAF WebACL with the ALB, using the same approach described in Pattern 1, Step 4.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="how-authentication-passes-through-the-alb"&gt;How authentication passes through the ALB&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The ALB passes the Authorization header through without modification. Both SigV4 signatures and OAuth Bearer tokens reach AgentCore transparently.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For SigV4 (AWS SDK), point the SDK to the ALB endpoint URL. The SDK handles signing automatically:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;import boto3

client = boto3.client(
    "bedrock-agentcore",
    endpoint_url="https://&amp;lt;alb-dns&amp;gt;"
)

response = client.invoke_agent_runtime(
    agentRuntimeArn="arn:aws:bedrock-agentcore:...",
    payload='{"input": "hello"}'
)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For OAuth (browser or curl), pass the JWT as a Bearer token:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;curl -X POST https://&amp;lt;alb-dns&amp;gt;/runtimes/&amp;lt;runtime-arn&amp;gt;/invocations \
  -H "Authorization: Bearer &amp;lt;jwt&amp;gt;" \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{"input": "hello"}'&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; When using the client_credentials grant in Amazon Cognito, the JWT contains a client_id claim but no aud claim. Configure the AgentCore OAuth authorizer with allowedClients (which matches client_id) instead of allowedAudience.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="pattern-2-trade-offs"&gt;Pattern 2 trade-offs&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;table style="width: 727px;height: 179px" border="1" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Aspect&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Impact&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Latency&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;No Lambda latency overhead, direct passthrough.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Cost&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;No additional compute cost.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Complexity&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Minimal components, ALB + VPC Endpoint only.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;Flexibility&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;No request transformation capability.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="closing-the-direct-access-backdoor-with-resource-policies"&gt;Closing the direct-access backdoor with resource policies&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Both patterns require a resource policy to help prevent direct access to AgentCore that bypasses AWS WAF. Without this policy, users with valid credentials can call the AgentCore public endpoint directly, circumventing AWS WAF protections.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div id="attachment_134633" style="width: 4139px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img aria-describedby="caption-attachment-134633" loading="lazy" class="size-full wp-image-134633" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/fig4-waf.png" alt="" width="4129" height="1659"&gt;
 &lt;p id="caption-attachment-134633" class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Without resource policy (left): direct access bypasses AWS WAF. With resource policy (right): only VPC Endpoint traffic is allowed.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The resource policy uses two statements. The first statement allows InvokeAgentRuntime only when the request originates from your specific VPC Endpoint (aws:SourceVpce condition). The second statement denies InvokeAgentRuntime for requests not originating from that VPC Endpoint, with aws:ViaAWSService set to false to avoid blocking internal AgentCore service calls.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Apply the policy using the put-resource-policy API:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following is an example resource-policy.json file:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "Version": "2012-10-17",
  "Statement": [
    {
      "Sid": "AllowVPCEOnly",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Principal": "*",
      "Action": "bedrock-agentcore:InvokeAgentRuntime",
      "Resource": "&amp;lt;runtime-arn&amp;gt;",
      "Condition": {
        "StringEquals": {
          "aws:SourceVpce": "&amp;lt;vpce-id&amp;gt;"
        }
      }
    },
    {
      "Sid": "DenyDirectAccess",
      "Effect": "Deny",
      "Principal": "*",
      "Action": "bedrock-agentcore:InvokeAgentRuntime",
      "Resource": "&amp;lt;runtime-arn&amp;gt;",
      "Condition": {
        "StringNotEquals": {
          "aws:SourceVpce": "&amp;lt;vpce-id&amp;gt;"
        },
        "Bool": {
          "aws:ViaAWSService": "false"
        }
      }
    }
  ]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws bedrock-agentcore-control put-resource-policy \
  --resource-arn &amp;lt;runtime-arn&amp;gt; \
  --policy file://resource-policy.json&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="verifying-the-resource-policy"&gt;Verifying the resource policy&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After applying the resource policy, verify that direct access is blocked and AWS WAF-routed access works. The following table shows the expected results:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table style="width: 100%" border="1" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Test&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Expected result&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;OAuth through ALB → VPC Endpoint&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;200 OK, request flows through WAF and VPC Endpoint&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;OAuth direct to AgentCore public endpoint&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;403, not authorized to perform bedrock-agentcore:InvokeAgentRuntime&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;SigV4 to OAuth-configured runtime&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;403, Authorization method mismatch&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;No authentication through ALB&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="288"&gt;401, Missing Authentication Token&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="defense-in-depth-security-layers"&gt;Defense in depth: Security layers&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Both patterns provide multiple layers of security. Each layer addresses a different threat vector, and together they form a defense-in-depth posture for your agent runtime. The following figure and table show how these layers stack from the outermost perimeter (AWS WAF) to the innermost control (VPC Endpoint Policy). A request must pass through every layer before reaching your agent runtime.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div id="attachment_134632" style="width: 3860px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img aria-describedby="caption-attachment-134632" loading="lazy" class="size-full wp-image-134632" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/fig5-waf.png" alt="" width="3850" height="1897"&gt;
 &lt;p id="caption-attachment-134632" class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Defense-in-depth layers, AWS WAF → ALB Security Group → VPC Endpoint Security Group → AgentCore Authentication → Resource Policy → VPC Endpoint Policy&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;table style="width: 100%" border="1" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Layer&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Protection&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Applies to&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;AWS WAF&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Rate limiting, SQL injection, XSS, bot control, geo-blocking&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Both patterns&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;ALB Security Group&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Network-level access control, HTTPS 443 only from allowed sources&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Both patterns&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;VPC Endpoint Security Group&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Restricts inbound to ALB security group only&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Both patterns&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;AgentCore Authentication&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;SigV4 or OAuth required on every invocation&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Both patterns&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Resource Policy&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Denies access not originating from the VPC Endpoint&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Both patterns&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;VPC Endpoint Policy (optional)&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Restricts to specific IAM principals or actions&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Both patterns&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="choosing-between-the-patterns"&gt;Choosing between the patterns&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following table summarizes the key differences to help you choose the right approach.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table style="width: 100%" border="1" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Criteria&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Pattern 1 (Lambda Proxy)&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Pattern 2 (Direct VPC Endpoint)&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Components&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;WAF + ALB + Lambda + VPC Endpoint&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;WAF + ALB + VPC Endpoint&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Additional latency&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;50–200 ms&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;No Lambda hop&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Additional cost&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Lambda invocations&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;None&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Request transformation&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Yes, full control&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;No&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Health check approach&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Lambda handles health&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Path / with matcher 200–499&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Authentication&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Lambda forwards token&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;ALB passes through transparently&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Complexity&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Medium&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Low&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;Start here if…&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;You need custom logic or multi-auth translation&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td width="192"&gt;You want the simplest, lowest-latency option&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before selecting a pattern, review the following considerations that apply to both architectures. These affect how you configure authentication, networking, and endpoint routing.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="important-considerations"&gt;Important considerations&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Port 443 only.&lt;/strong&gt; The VPC Endpoint exposes only HTTPS 443. Port 8080 (the container’s internal port) is not accessible through the VPC Endpoint. AgentCore routes 443 → 8080 internally.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Private DNS not required.&lt;/strong&gt; The ALB targets VPC Endpoint ENI IPs directly, so Private DNS on the VPC Endpoint is not required for either pattern.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;OAuth blocks SigV4.&lt;/strong&gt; When an AgentCore Runtime is configured with an OAuth authorizer, SigV4 requests are rejected with “Authorization method mismatch.” Choose one authentication method per runtime.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;VPC Endpoint service selection.&lt;/strong&gt; Use bedrock-agentcore for Runtime data plane. The bedrock-agentcore.gateway endpoint is for AgentCore Gateway only and will not route to your Runtime.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;ENI IP stability.&lt;/strong&gt; VPC Endpoint ENI IPs are stable but can change if the endpoint is recreated. Monitor target health and update targets if IPs change.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="cleanup"&gt;Cleanup&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you created resources while following along, delete them to avoid ongoing charges. Remove the resource policy first, then disassociate the AWS WAF WebACL from the ALB. Delete the ALB (listener and target groups first, then the load balancer), the VPC Interface Endpoint, and associated security groups. For Pattern 1, delete the Lambda function. If you created a test AgentCore Runtime or Amazon Cognito User Pool, note that deleting these permanently removes agent configurations and users respectively.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For a detailed cleanup sequence with commands, see the repository &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-bedrock-agentcore-waf-patterns/blob/main/README.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;README&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You now have two tested architecture patterns for placing AWS WAF in front of Amazon Bedrock AgentCore Runtime. Pattern 2 (direct VPC Endpoint) has fewer components, no Lambda latency overhead, and no extra cost for most use cases. Pattern 1 (Lambda proxy) adds flexibility when you need request transformation or custom authentication logic between the ALB and AgentCore.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Regardless of which pattern you choose, apply a resource policy to close the direct-access backdoor so that traffic flows through AWS WAF. Combined with VPC Endpoint security groups and the built-in authentication in AgentCore, these patterns provide defense in depth for your AI agent deployments.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To learn more, explore the following resources:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-bedrock-agentcore-waf-patterns/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;GitHub repository&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/what-is.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore documentation&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/vpc-interface-endpoints.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;VPC Interface Endpoints for AgentCore&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/resource-based-policies.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Resource-based policies for AgentCore&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/waf/latest/developerguide/what-is-aws-waf.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS WAF Developer Guide&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/gateway-waf.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Protecting your gateway with AWS WAF&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt;
