		<item>
		<title>Monitoring discriminative ML models using Amazon SageMaker AI with MLflow</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/monitoring-discriminative-ml-models-using-amazon-sagemaker-ai-with-mlflow/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Sandeep Raveesh-Babu]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 07 Jul 2026 16:49:09 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon EventBridge]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker AI]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Simple Notification Service (SNS)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Simple Storage Service (S3)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">7d4027959d55efce26bfb96736a23fc3eff1bebb</guid>

					<description>Implementing a data and model monitoring solution is necessary to maintain prediction accuracy and help achieve the best outcome for your machine learning use case. This post shows how you can use open source Evidently together with Amazon SageMaker AI to generate monitoring reports, organize and compare the results in MLflow, scale through pipelines, and trigger drift notifications.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;The effectiveness and accuracy of machine learning (ML) models decreases almost as soon as the training job finishes. Changes in consumer behavior, releases of new products, upgrades in sensor technology, and a shifting economic and political landscape are all examples of uncontrollable factors that change the patterns and probabilities the model learned during training. By actively monitoring models deployed in production for changes in accuracy and baseline statistics, you can intervene before the drop in accuracy becomes problematic. Model monitoring can be combined with AI observability tools that track latency, application availability, and other metrics used to identify problems in the overall system.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This post focuses on discriminative machine learning models used for classification and regression use cases. For generative AI models, see &lt;a href="https://builder.aws.com/content/39pAP53j9mlK9o1NBBjWZ4HMJPl/production-ready-real-time-monitoring-solution-for-llms-on-amazon-sagemaker-ai-endpoint-inference" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Production-Ready Real-Time Monitoring Solution for LLMs on Amazon SageMaker AI Endpoint inference&lt;/a&gt;. The factors that cause a reduction in quality for discriminative ML models can be broadly split into two categories:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Data drift&lt;/strong&gt; refers to changes in the statistical properties of the input data. It can be as simple as an unexpected change in an upstream data source that changes a column from integer to float data type, or as complex as entirely new product lines being released. You can measure data drift by calculating baseline statistics for the training dataset and comparing these to the same statistics calculated on data gathered over time in production.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Model drift&lt;/strong&gt; refers to changes in the accuracy of predictions produced by the model because the probabilistic patterns learned by the model no longer fit the data coming in. This can be caused, for example, by changes in consumer behavior because of an improving economy. You can measure model drift by gathering the ground truth labels to calculate model quality metrics, and comparing these metrics to the same metrics calculated during the model training process.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-20699-1.png" alt="Conceptual diagram illustrating data drift and model drift. Training data is used to train a model, which is deployed to a SageMaker AI endpoint. Data drift is detected by comparing the distribution of inference input data against the original training data distribution. Model drift is detected by comparing inference output predictions against ground truth data. Dashed orange arrows indicate the comparison flows for both types of drift." width="600" height="283"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 1: How data drift and model drift fit in an ML workflow&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI&lt;/a&gt; is a fully managed machine learning service that helps organizations build, train, deploy, and manage both discriminative and generative ML models. Although SageMaker AI is fully managed, you might need a more customizable approach. For example, you might want to manage the entire modeling life cycle cost-effectively, monitor unique use cases that managed services do not support, or integrate your model monitoring into other UI or observability pipelines. Therefore, this post introduces a model monitoring architecture based on the open source &lt;a href="https://www.evidentlyai.com/evidently-oss" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Evidently Python library&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/mlflow.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI with MLflow&lt;/a&gt; for calculating data and model drift. The results of this model monitoring solution can be integrated into your preferred dashboard, can be used to send alerts to relevant stakeholders, or can trigger automatic model retraining pipelines.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-overview"&gt;Solution overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This solution demonstrates how to implement model monitoring in the machine learning workflow, from model training to model deployment. Figure 2 shows the workflow for a batch inference use case, up to and including alerting in Slack and visualizing the model results in MLflow.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-20699-2.png" alt="Architecture diagram showing batch inference monitoring on SageMaker AI. Training data flows from S3 through a SageMaker training job to produce a model. A scheduled SageMaker pipeline runs Batch Transform for inference followed by a Monitoring Job. Baseline data is stored in S3 and compared against batch outputs. EventBridge Scheduler triggers the pipeline on a schedule. Monitoring results flow to the SageMaker AI MLflow App for data drift and quality, model quality, and metrics reporting. Amazon SNS sends alerts via email or Slack. Users are notified about training data updates, retraining needs, and model updates." width="600" height="761"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 2: Model monitoring workflow for batch inference&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The workflow consists of the following steps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/how-it-works-training.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;training job&lt;/a&gt; to train the model on input data from an Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3) bucket. The same training job calculates model metrics, which can be stored in MLflow. The baseline dataset used to train the model is stored separately in S3 so it can be used to monitor the model in production.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/batch-transform.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Batch transform&lt;/a&gt; for inference on production workloads. The results of a batch transform job are stored in an S3 bucket.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The results of the batch transform job, and the original dataset and model metrics, are used in a &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/processing-job.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;processing job&lt;/a&gt; to calculate data drift and model quality metrics using open source Evidently presets. Note that Evidently calculates model metrics. It does not calculate model drift directly by comparing the metrics to the previous metrics from the training run. However, the processing job can be extended with custom code to calculate model drift. The batch transform and processing job can be wrapped in a &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/pipelines-overview.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;pipeline&lt;/a&gt; for scheduling using &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/eventbridge/latest/userguide/using-eventbridge-scheduler.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon EventBridge Scheduler&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;All monitoring metrics and Evidently reports are stored in &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/mlflow-app-setup.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;MLflow&lt;/a&gt; where you can track runs over time, compare results between runs, and visualize the report.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Optionally, if drift is detected, the pipeline can trigger an &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/prometheus/latest/userguide/AMP-alertmanager-SNS-otherdestinations.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Simple Notification Service (Amazon SNS) notification&lt;/a&gt; to alert users by email.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can take a similar approach for use cases where &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/realtime-endpoints.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;real-time endpoints&lt;/a&gt; are used for model deployment. Figure 3 shows the workflow for real-time endpoints. The key difference is that the endpoint must have &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/model-monitor-data-capture.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;data capture enabled&lt;/a&gt;, which logs the inputs and outputs of the endpoint to an S3 bucket. Instead of a processing job, this architecture uses &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/lambda/latest/dg/welcome.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Lambda&lt;/a&gt; functions to deploy the monitoring code. Both architectures can use either processing jobs or Lambda, based on your preference. The AWS Lambda functions that calculate data and model drift can run on a regular schedule. You can also trigger them when data from the endpoint lands in the S3 bucket.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-20699-3.png" alt="Architecture diagram showing real-time inference monitoring on SageMaker AI. Training data flows from S3 through a SageMaker AI training job to produce a model deployed on a SageMaker AI endpoint. The endpoint triggers DataCapture, writing inference data as JSONL to an S3 Data Capture bucket. A Data and Model Monitoring component uses AWS Lambda triggered by EventBridge S3 events and EventBridge Scheduler to compare captured data against baseline data in S3 and ground truth data. Monitoring results are logged to the SageMaker AI MLflow App for data drift and quality, model quality, and metrics reporting. Amazon SNS sends alerts to Slack when issues are detected. Users receive notifications for training data updates, retraining needs, and model updates." width="600" height="761"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 3: Model monitoring workflow for real-time endpoints&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This pattern for real-time endpoints can also be used with &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker Hyperpod&lt;/a&gt;, where you can provision clusters to use for model training as well as model inference. You can &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-data-capture.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;enable data capture for Hyperpod&lt;/a&gt; at the endpoint level, at the load balancer level, or at the model pod level. Inference requests and responses are automatically written to an S3 bucket, where you can access them to perform model monitoring.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Both previous diagrams focus only on the model monitoring process. However, model monitoring is normally implemented as part of a larger MLOps workflow. Figure 4 shows how this model monitoring solution fits in an end-to-end MLOps scenario, where the processing job used to calculate drift is deployed alongside the endpoint through a CI/CD workflow. For more information on implementing MLOps with SageMaker AI, see &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/mlops-foundation-roadmap-for-enterprises-with-amazon-sagemaker/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;MLOps foundation roadmap for enterprises with Amazon SageMaker AI&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div id="attachment_134556" style="width: 1930px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img aria-describedby="caption-attachment-134556" loading="lazy" class="size-full wp-image-134556" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/Screenshot-2026-06-30-at-6.53.09 AM.png" alt="" width="1920" height="988"&gt;
 &lt;p id="caption-attachment-134556" class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 4: How model monitoring integrates with an MLOps architecture&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="walkthrough"&gt;Walkthrough&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This section provides a step-by-step explanation for setting up the model monitoring solution for batch transform. See the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-aiops-on-amazon-sagemakerai/tree/main/monitoring" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;full repository&lt;/a&gt; for other examples, including a sample for &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-aiops-on-amazon-sagemakerai/tree/main/monitoring/predictiveml-endpoint-monitoring" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;real-time endpoints&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before following the steps in this walkthrough, you will need:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/gs.html?icmpid=docs_sagemaker_lp/index.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI domain&lt;/a&gt; in an AWS Region where this service is available.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/mlflow-app-setup.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;MLflow App&lt;/a&gt; in SageMaker Studio.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/studio-updated-jl-user-guide-create-space.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;JupyterLab space&lt;/a&gt; within SageMaker Studio (an &lt;code&gt;ml.t3.medium&lt;/code&gt; instance is sufficient for this solution).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A copy of the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-aiops-on-amazon-sagemakerai/tree/main/monitoring/predictiveml-batch-monitoring-pipeline" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;solution repository&lt;/a&gt; on JupyterLab. Clone the repository after the space has started.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To continue with the walkthrough, open the notebook &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-aiops-on-amazon-sagemakerai/blob/main/monitoring/predictiveml-batch-monitoring-pipeline/predictive_ml_experimentation_data_model_monitoring_evidently.ipynb" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;predictive_ml_experimentation_data_model_monitoring_evidently.ipynb&lt;/a&gt;. Note that the solution uses the &lt;a href="https://sagemaker.readthedocs.io/en/stable/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SageMaker Python SDK v3&lt;/a&gt;. If you use the &lt;code&gt;DefaultMLFlowApp&lt;/code&gt;, the notebook code identifies the correct app automatically. Otherwise, edit the code with the name of your MLflow App.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="model-training-and-inference"&gt;Model training and inference&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The example notebook uses the &lt;a href="https://archive.ics.uci.edu/dataset/222/bank+marketing" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Bank Marketing dataset&lt;/a&gt; from the UCI Machine Learning Repository. This dataset contains information about marketing phone calls made on behalf of a Portuguese bank, with the goal of predicting if the customer will subscribe to a term deposit. It is a binary classification use case where the target variable has a value of 1 if the customer subscribed and 0 if a customer did not subscribe.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In the first half of the notebook, the data is cleaned, processed, and split into training, validation, and test sets. An XGBoost model is trained on the training and validation datasets, with logs and metrics sent to MLflow and the final model object registered in the MLflow model registry.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Two key actions in this data processing and model training section set up the model for monitoring later in production:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The training dataset is stored as the baseline dataset in an S3 bucket, which you use to calculate data drift.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The model metrics are stored in MLflow, which can be used for model drift calculations.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-20699-5.png" alt="Screenshot of the MLflow Model Metrics tab for a training run “training_bank-marketing-prediction-xgb-ml”. Three metric charts are displayed: precision (0.68 shown as a bar chart), recall (0.56 shown as a bar chart), and validation-auc (shown as a line chart starting around 0.933 and converging to approximately 0.945 over 80 training steps with auto-refresh enabled)." width="600" height="556"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 5: Model metrics (precision, recall, AUC) displayed in MLflow&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Next, the notebook creates the model object from the training job output and sets up a batch transform job which runs the test features through the model for inference.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="calculating-data-drift-and-model-quality"&gt;Calculating data drift and model quality&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Evidently has various presets for calculating different types of &lt;a href="https://docs.evidentlyai.com/metrics/preset_data_drift" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;data drift&lt;/a&gt;, all of which can be customized to suit the particular ML use case. The sample notebook uses the &lt;code&gt;DataDriftPreset&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;DataSummaryPreset&lt;/code&gt; and creates a helper function to save the Evidently reports (both HTML and JSON) in MLflow and extracts specific drift values to save separately as metrics in MLflow. This enables you to compare different runs or generate alerts based on certain values.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-20699-6.png" alt="Screenshot of an Evidently data drift report rendered as an MLflow artifact. The header states “Dataset Drift is NOT detected” with a threshold of 0.5. Summary statistics show 20 columns total, 2 drifted columns, and 0.1 share of drifted columns. The Data Drift Summary table shows drift detected for 10% of columns (2 out of 20), with details for the “age” column (numeric type) showing reference and current distribution histograms, detected drift using Wasserstein distance (normed) with a score of 0.202105." width="600" height="793"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 6: An Evidently data drift report as an artifact in MLflow&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-20699-7.png" alt="Screenshot of an MLflow run detail page for “data_drift_quality_monitoring_20260506_151724”. The Overview tab shows a metrics table with DriftedColumnsCount.count (2), DriftedColumnsCount.share (0.1), ValueDrift:age (0.202), and ValueDrift:duration (0.169). Below, a Parameters section lists five parameters: reference_data_size (28831), current_data_size (6179), monitoring_timestamp (2026-05-06T15:17:24), model_name (test-trad-xgb-mp-20260506144853), and training_job (bank-marketing-xgboost)." width="600" height="790"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 7: Data drift metrics and parameters in MLflow&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Another advantage of saving each data drift calculation as a run in MLflow is that it allows us to add parameters such as the model name, the name of the training job, the size of the dataset, and other important information.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Evidently also provides presets for calculating model quality. This notebook uses the &lt;code&gt;ClassificationPreset&lt;/code&gt;, which calculates accuracy, precision, recall, F1 score, and more based on model predictions and the ground truth dataset. Instead of the &lt;code&gt;ClassificationPreset&lt;/code&gt; with its broad range of metrics, Evidently also supports &lt;a href="https://docs.evidentlyai.com/docs/library/report" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;custom reports&lt;/a&gt; that you can target to calculate the metrics most relevant to a particular use case. For example, because of imbalanced labels in the marketing dataset used in the sample notebook, accuracy is a less relevant metric than precision, recall, and AUC.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Similar to data monitoring, the Evidently reports for model quality are added as artifacts to MLflow, and the individual metrics can be extracted into MLflow metrics. Note that Evidently does not calculate model drift, meaning the difference between metrics from the original training job and metrics calculated from the predictions. However, you can add drift calculations in the monitoring code, and we provide an example of this in the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-aiops-on-amazon-sagemakerai/tree/main/monitoring/predictiveml-endpoint-monitoring" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;real-time endpoint sample&lt;/a&gt;, where model drift, when detected, triggers an alert via Amazon SNS.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-20699-8.png" alt="Screenshot of an Evidently classification report rendered as an MLflow artifact. The report shows four key metrics: Accuracy 0.917, Precision 0.651, Recall 0.57, and F1 0.608. Below is a confusion matrix heatmap with actual values on the y-axis and predicted values on the x-axis. True negatives: 5270, false positives: 213, false negatives: 397, true positives: 299. The color scale ranges from dark blue (low counts) to red (high counts around 5000)." width="600" height="768"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 8: Model quality report artifact in MLflow&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Views in MLflow can be customized and individual runs can be compared based on certain attributes. Figure 9 shows a view of multiple data drift, model quality, and comprehensive quality runs together with the original training job. You can see which training run the monitoring runs relate to, how many drifted columns were detected in the consecutive data drift runs, and how much the accuracy dropped between training and running the model quality job.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-20699-9.png" alt="Screenshot of the MLflow experiment runs table view showing five runs filtered by metrics.rmse &lt; 1 and params.model = “tree”. Columns display Run Name, Created time, Duration, Accuracy, and DriftedColumns count. Runs include data_drift_quality_monitoring (6.6s, 2 drifted columns), comprehensive_monitoring (7.8s), model_quality (5.9s, accuracy 0.917), another data_drift_quality_monitoring run (7.2s, 2 drifted columns), and training_test-trad-xgb-mp (17.2s, accuracy 0.921). All runs were created 21-22 hours ago with active state." width="600" height="388"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 9: Multiple monitoring runs in MLflow&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Furthermore, you can plot the metrics captured in MLflow over time. As shown in Figure 10, this lets you view the entire model life cycle in one place, covering model experimentation, model training, and model deployment and monitoring.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-20699-10.png" alt="Screenshot of the MLflow experiment tracking UI in SageMaker Studio showing a chart view of model metrics across multiple runs. The experiment “demo-trad-ml-xgb-train-monitor” displays 22 model metrics as horizontal bar charts, including Accuracy, DriftedColumnsCount.count, DriftedColumnsCount.share, F1ByLabel for labels 0 and 1, and F1Score. Multiple runs are listed in the left panel including model_quality, data_drift_quality_monitoring, and training runs, color-coded in the charts. Runs are filtered by metrics.rmse &lt; 1 and params.model = “tree”." width="600" height="1766"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 10: Model life cycle tracking metrics view in MLflow&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="scaling-with-pipelines"&gt;Scaling with pipelines&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Although the first notebook demonstrates in depth how to train a model, deploy it, and run monitoring with Evidently, the second notebook &lt;code&gt;batch_monitoring_pipeline.ipynb&lt;/code&gt; shows how to scale inference and monitoring through jobs and pipelines. It creates a pipeline with two steps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/build-and-manage-steps-types.html#step-type-transform" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Transform step&lt;/a&gt; that fetches the input data from S3 and runs the data through the trained XGBoost model to generate the subscription probability predictions. Predictions are stored back in S3 and passed to the next step.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/build-and-manage-steps-types.html#step-type-processing" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Processing step&lt;/a&gt; that uses the predictions and the baseline dataset to perform monitoring. The input dataset used to generate predictions is compared to the baseline dataset to calculate data drift, and the reports are added to MLflow.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 610px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-20699-11.png" alt="Screenshot of a successful SageMaker pipeline execution (execution-1778239106370) showing a two-step graph view. The first step, BatchTransform, deploys the model for batch inference and shows a green success checkmark. The second step, EvidentlyMonitoringStep, processes data for monitoring and also shows a green success checkmark. The steps are connected sequentially with an arrow." width="600" height="699"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 11: A pipeline with one batch transform step and one monitoring step&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Finally, the notebook demonstrates how pipeline executions can be run on a regular schedule with Amazon EventBridge. For an example of how to trigger pipeline executions based on inference data becoming available in S3, see the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-aiops-on-amazon-sagemakerai/tree/main/monitoring/predictiveml-endpoint-monitoring" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;real-time endpoint sample&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="clean-up"&gt;Clean up&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-aiops-on-amazon-sagemakerai/blob/main/monitoring/predictiveml-batch-monitoring-pipeline/predictive_ml_experimentation_data_model_monitoring_evidently.ipynb" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;notebook&lt;/a&gt; has a section at the end to help you clean up the resources you deployed, including any endpoints and model objects. If you wish to delete the data artifacts in S3 or the runs logged to MLflow, you need to remove them manually.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Implementing a data and model monitoring solution is necessary to maintain prediction accuracy and help achieve the best outcome for your machine learning use case. This post shows how you can use open source Evidently together with Amazon SageMaker AI to generate monitoring reports, organize and compare the results in MLflow, scale through pipelines, and trigger drift notifications. The &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-aiops-on-amazon-sagemakerai/tree/main/monitoring" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS sample GitHub repository&lt;/a&gt; provides implementations for both batch predictions and real-time endpoints, with scaling done either through SageMaker AI pipelines or AWS Lambda. To learn more about MLOps on Amazon SageMaker AI, see the &lt;a href="https://catalog.workshops.aws/mlops-from-idea-to-production/en-US" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI MLOps workshop&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;hr&gt;

		<item>
		<title>Build an AI-powered AWS support companion with Amazon Bedrock AgentCore</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/build-an-ai-powered-aws-support-companion-with-amazon-bedrock-agentcore/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Jose Soto]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 07 Jul 2026 16:46:43 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock AgentCore]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon CloudWatch]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[AWS Lambda]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">efafaf3705d5c88f85652f58ee940189897972c4</guid>

					<description>In this post, you build an AWS Support Companion using Amazon Bedrock AgentCore. The agent uses Strands Agents as the orchestration framework and connects to AWS services through the Model Context Protocol (MCP). By the end, you have a working agent that can analyze CloudWatch logs, search AWS documentation, query community knowledge from AWS re:Post, and create support cases, all from a single conversational interface. The solution deploys with a single script using AWS CloudFormation and includes a web frontend built on AWS Amplify for interacting with the agent.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Managing AWS infrastructure often means switching between consoles, searching documentation, and manually creating support cases. For each incident, an engineer opens the AWS Management Console, checks &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cloudwatch/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon CloudWatch&lt;/a&gt;, searches AWS documentation, reviews community posts, and files a support case. This context-switching adds up to 30–45 minutes per investigation before resolution work begins.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, you build an AWS Support Companion using &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/agents-tools-runtime.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore&lt;/a&gt;. The agent uses &lt;a href="https://strandsagents.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Strands Agents&lt;/a&gt; as the orchestration framework and connects to AWS services through the &lt;a href="https://modelcontextprotocol.io/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Model Context Protocol (MCP)&lt;/a&gt;. By the end, you have a working agent that can analyze CloudWatch logs, search AWS documentation, query community knowledge from &lt;a href="https://repost.aws/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS re:Post&lt;/a&gt;, and create support cases, all from a single conversational interface. The solution deploys with a single script using &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cloudformation/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS CloudFormation&lt;/a&gt; and includes a web frontend built on &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/amplify/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Amplify&lt;/a&gt; for interacting with the agent.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-19981-1.png" alt="Architecture diagram of the AWS Support Companion solution" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="the-incident-investigation-bottleneck"&gt;The incident investigation bottleneck&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;AWS support and operations teams follow a repetitive pattern for every incident:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Open the AWS Management Console and navigate to the affected service.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Check CloudWatch logs and metrics for error patterns.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Search AWS documentation for relevant troubleshooting guidance.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Review community posts on AWS re:Post for similar issues.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Compile findings and create a support case with the appropriate severity.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Attach evidence and context to the case.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each step requires a different tool and interface. Manual investigation limits the speed at which teams can respond, and the context gathered in one tool does not carry over to the next.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-overview"&gt;Solution overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The solution consolidates these steps into a single conversational agent deployed on Amazon Bedrock AgentCore. AgentCore handles the operational complexity of running a production AI agent (session isolation, auto scaling, security, and observability), so you focus on what the agent does rather than how it runs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The agent connects to the following components:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Agent runtime –&lt;/strong&gt; A Python application using Strands Agents, packaged as a Docker container and deployed to AgentCore Runtime. The agent orchestrates calls to a foundation model (FM) (Amazon Nova Pro through Amazon Bedrock) and tools based on your input. You can swap to supported models without changing the agent code.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;MCP servers –&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;Three MCP servers give the agent access to AWS documentation (&lt;code&gt;aws-documentation-mcp-server&lt;/code&gt;), &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/premiumsupport/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Support&lt;/a&gt; APIs (&lt;code&gt;aws-support-mcp-server&lt;/code&gt;), and AWS service APIs (&lt;code&gt;aws-api-mcp-server&lt;/code&gt;). MCP provides a standard protocol for AI agents to receive context from external tools.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;AgentCore Gateway –&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;Centralizes tools into reusable, secure endpoints. The gateway provides access to AWS re:Post for community knowledge through an &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/lambda/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Lambda&lt;/a&gt;-backed target with &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cognito/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Cognito&lt;/a&gt; JSON Web Token (JWT) authentication.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;AgentCore Memory –&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;Maintains short-term conversation context so the agent builds on previous troubleshooting steps within a session.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;API and frontend layers –&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/api-gateway/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon API Gateway&lt;/a&gt; with Cognito authorization, &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/waf/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS WAF&lt;/a&gt; rate limiting, and request validation fronts a Lambda function that invokes the AgentCore Runtime. An AWS Amplify-hosted React application provides the user interface with Cognito authentication for sign-up and sign-in.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Guardrails –&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;An &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/guardrails.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock Guardrails&lt;/a&gt; configuration filters harmful content, helps block prompt injection attacks, redacts personally identifiable information (PII) such as AWS keys, credit cards, and Social Security numbers, and restricts the agent to AWS support topics.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Infrastructure –&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;A single CloudFormation template deploys all resources, including AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) roles, Cognito user pools, &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/kms/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Key Management Service (AWS KMS)&lt;/a&gt; keys, &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/secrets-manager/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Secrets Manager&lt;/a&gt; secrets, and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/systems-manager/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Systems Manager Parameter Store&lt;/a&gt; parameters.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-19981-2.png" alt="Component diagram showing the agent runtime, MCP servers, AgentCore Gateway, API Gateway, and Amplify frontend" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before you begin, confirm that you have the following:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Python 3.11 or later.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Docker with buildx support for ARM64.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AWS account with permissions for Amazon Bedrock, IAM, and CloudFormation.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Amazon Bedrock model access for Amazon Nova Pro in your deployment AWS Region (you can use a different supported model).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/cli/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Command Line Interface (AWS CLI)&lt;/a&gt; installed and configured with appropriate credentials.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The &lt;a href="https://docs.astral.sh/uv/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;uv&lt;/a&gt; package manager installed.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AWS Business, Enterprise On-Ramp, or Enterprise Support plan (required for the Support MCP server).&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Important:&lt;/strong&gt; This solution creates billable AWS resources. Follow the Cleanup section at the end of this post to avoid ongoing charges.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="deploy-the-solution"&gt;Deploy the solution&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The deployment uses a single script that provisions all infrastructure, builds the container, and outputs the configuration values you need.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-1-clone-the-repository"&gt;Step 1: Clone the repository&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Start by cloning the sample code and changing into the project directory:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;git clone https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-support-agent-with-agentcore
cd sample-support-agent-with-agentcore&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-2-configure-aws-credentials"&gt;Step 2: Configure AWS credentials&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Confirm your AWS CLI is configured with credentials that have the required permissions:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws sts get-caller-identity&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-3-run-the-deployment"&gt;Step 3: Run the deployment&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Make the deploy script executable and run it with the container build flag:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;chmod +x deploy.sh
./deploy.sh --build-container&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The script handles Python environment setup with uv, pre-deployment security validation (AWS CloudTrail check, template validation, credential type check), Amazon Elastic Container Registry (Amazon ECR) repository creation, Docker image build, and CloudFormation stack deployment. It verifies that CloudTrail is active in the target Region, validates the CloudFormation template, and confirms you are using temporary role credentials rather than long-term IAM user keys.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When the stack completes, the script outputs the configuration values that you need for the frontend: Cognito User Pool ID, Cognito Client ID, Cognito Identity Pool ID, and Agent Runtime Amazon Resource Name (ARN).&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-19981-3.png" alt="Terminal output showing a successful CloudFormation deployment with configuration values" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-4-deploy-the-amplify-frontend"&gt;Step 4: Deploy the Amplify frontend&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Navigate to the AWS Amplify console in your deployment Region, then follow these steps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Select the &lt;strong&gt;support-agent-frontend&lt;/strong&gt; app.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;Deploy updates&lt;/strong&gt; in the main branch.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Select &lt;strong&gt;Drag and drop&lt;/strong&gt; as the method, then upload the &lt;code&gt;support-agent-amplify-frontend.zip&lt;/code&gt; file from the &lt;code&gt;frontend/&lt;/code&gt; directory.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;Save and deploy&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-5-configure-and-access-the-application"&gt;Step 5: Configure and access the application&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Update the frontend configuration with the CloudFormation output values (User Pool ID, Client ID, Identity Pool ID, and Runtime ARN). Create a user account through the Sign Up option, verify your email with the one-time code, and sign in.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-19981-4.png" alt="Support Companion web interface showing the chat panel after sign-in" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-6-interact-with-the-agent"&gt;Step 6: Interact with the agent&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The chat interface connects to the agent running in AgentCore Runtime. The agent has access to AWS MCP servers for documentation and support, and AWS re:Post through AgentCore Gateway. Try queries like:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“What are the best practices for securing an Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3) bucket?”&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“Help me troubleshoot a Lambda function timeout in us-east-1”&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“Create a support case for an intermittent connectivity issue with my RDS instance”&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/24/ML-19981-5.gif" alt="Agent responding to a troubleshooting query in the chat interface" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="understanding-the-agent-code"&gt;Understanding the agent code&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The agent is defined in &lt;code&gt;agent.py&lt;/code&gt; and uses the &lt;code&gt;BedrockAgentCoreApp&lt;/code&gt; framework. This section highlights three implementation patterns that address common challenges with production agents.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Parallel MCP initialization.&lt;/strong&gt; Loading three MCP servers sequentially adds noticeable startup latency. The agent initializes all three clients in parallel using &lt;code&gt;asyncio.gather&lt;/code&gt;, which reduces cold start time. Each client connects to its server using &lt;code&gt;uvx&lt;/code&gt; and registers the available tools with the Strands Agent:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;# Initialize all clients in parallel
results = await asyncio.gather(
    init_aws_docs(),    # AWS Documentation MCP
    init_aws_support(), # AWS Support MCP
    init_aws_api(),     # AWS API MCP
    return_exceptions=True
)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Memory with graceful timeout.&lt;/strong&gt; The agent uses AgentCore Memory to maintain conversation context. On each request, it retrieves the last three conversation turns with a two-second timeout. If context retrieval times out, the agent proceeds without it rather than blocking the response. After generating a response, it saves the conversation asynchronously. Memory is scoped per session ID, so each user conversation maintains its own context.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Gateway token refresh.&lt;/strong&gt; The AgentCore Gateway client authenticates using Cognito JWT tokens stored in Secrets Manager. The agent validates token expiration before each gateway call and refreshes automatically when the token is within five minutes of expiring. Without this, gateway calls fail intermittently after the one-hour token lifetime.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="security-considerations"&gt;Security considerations&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The solution deploys with multiple security layers configured by default. Authentication uses Amazon Cognito with NIST SP 800-63B-aligned password policies. AWS WAF with rate limiting and managed rule sets protects the API Gateway. Each component has its own least-privilege IAM role, and the agent invoker Lambda redacts credential patterns from responses before returning them to users.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Bedrock Guardrails filters harmful content, blocks prompt injection (set to HIGH), redacts PII (AWS keys, credit cards, SSNs), and restricts the agent to AWS support topics. API Gateway access logs flow to Amazon CloudWatch with 90-day retention, and AWS CloudTrail captures API calls. For the full security implementation details, including encryption configuration, IAM role scoping, and guardrail policies, see &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-support-agent-with-agentcore/blob/main/SECURITY.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SECURITY.md&lt;/a&gt; in the repository.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="cleanup"&gt;Cleanup&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To avoid ongoing charges, delete the resources in this order.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Warning: &lt;/strong&gt;these steps are irreversible. Back up any data you need before proceeding.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the Amplify app from the AWS Amplify console.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the CloudFormation stack: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws cloudformation delete-stack --stack-name bedrock-agentcore-stack --region us-west-2&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the ECR repository (CloudFormation doesn’t delete non-empty repositories): 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws ecr delete-repository --repository-name support-agent --region us-west-2 --force&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the S3 bucket used for CloudFormation templates: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws s3 rb s3://cfn-templates-ACCOUNTID-us-west-2 --force&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, you built an AI-powered AWS Support Companion using Amazon Bedrock AgentCore. The agent combines foundation model reasoning with real-time access to AWS documentation, support APIs, and community knowledge through MCP and AgentCore Gateway. The solution deploys with a single script and includes authentication, rate limiting, encryption, guardrails, and audit logging.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The agent consolidates multiple investigation tools into a single conversational interface, so engineers can move from problem identification to support case creation without switching between consoles.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The sample repository includes additional guidance on network isolation, multi-account deployment, observability with AgentCore tracing, human escalation thresholds, and secret rotation for production readiness. To get started, visit the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/agents-tools-runtime.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore documentation&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-support-agent-with-agentcore" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;clone the sample repository&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you need a fully managed incident investigation service, see &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/devops-agent/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS DevOps Agent&lt;/a&gt;. This post shows you how to build a custom agent on AgentCore Runtime when you need full control over the tools, model, guardrails, and workflow.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;hr&gt;

		<title>How AWS Finance teams reclaimed hundreds of hours with Amazon Quick</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/how-aws-finance-teams-reclaimed-hundreds-of-hours-with-amazon-quick/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Sindhu Chandra]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 07 Jul 2026 16:43:11 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon Quick Suite]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Artificial Intelligence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Customer Solutions]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">d2a346e6e832a28be3be2452e6ff0d56aa698fff</guid>

					<description>In this post, we show how AWS Finance used chat agents and Flows in Amazin Quick to transform two of their most time-consuming workflows.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Every finance professional knows the drill. Monday morning arrives, and your Financial Planning and Analysis (FP&amp;amp;A) team disappears into data compilation. They pull numbers from multiple systems, reconcile sources, build charts, and write commentary. All to answer a question that should be straightforward: &lt;em&gt;what happened with revenue last week, and why?&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Across AWS Finance, teams were spending hundreds of hours a month on exactly this kind of work. Not analysis. Not strategy. Getting the data ready so the real work could begin.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Quick&lt;/a&gt; is a generative AI assistant that connects across all your enterprise data and applications, so business users can search, analyze, and take action through natural language. It handles the complexity of querying millions of rows, running advanced analytics, and automating recurring workflows so your team doesn’t need to.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we show how AWS Finance used &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/chat-agents/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;chat agents&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/flows/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Flows&lt;/a&gt; in Quick to transform two of their most time-consuming workflows.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="use-case-1-scenario-modeling-and-risk-analysis-across-the-strategic-portfolio"&gt;Use case 1: Scenario modeling and risk analysis across the strategic portfolio&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Setting financial targets for strategic customers requires reconciling bottom-up forecasts from business teams with top-down projections from leadership. It also demands enough depth to catch the risks hiding beneath historical data.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The team built an Amazon Quick &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/chat-agents/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;chat agent&lt;/a&gt; that connects directly to enterprise data sources and delivers sophisticated insights through natural language conversation. The agent queries millions of rows across Amazon Redshift data tables instantly while also searching external data signals.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/23/ML-20844-1.png" alt="Quick presenting a scenario analysis and creating a 5-sheet Microsoft Excel worksheet." width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Screenshot showing Quick presenting a scenario analysis and creating a 5-sheet Microsoft Excel worksheet.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Here’s what changed:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Before:&lt;/strong&gt; Analysts could deep-dive roughly a third of strategic customers in the time available between bottoms-up inputs and when top-level targets are due. The rest got surface-level coverage. A single customer analysis consumed up to 6 hours of manual work, including extracting data, running models, and documenting findings.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;After:&lt;/strong&gt; The Quick agent evaluates statistical forecasts, runs regression analysis, Monte Carlo simulations, and performs scenario modeling across multiple factors in approximately 10 minutes per customer. It surfaces risks and opportunities that manual analysis missed. The team now covers their entire customer portfolio with even greater depth than before.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;blockquote&gt;
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;“We have expanded from deep-diving a third of our strategic customers to covering our entire portfolio. Our finance team now spends time on what matters: partnering with the business to drive revenue, not compiling data or writing complex queries.”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/blockquote&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;— Geoff Winkler&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;What makes this work:&lt;/strong&gt; An analyst asks a question in natural language: “Run an opportunity and risk assessment for our top strategic accounts.” Quick then queries millions of rows, runs advanced analytics, and synthesizes structured data with unstructured insights from field reports and pipeline data. The agent does bull versus bear analysis by reviewing accounts with upside potential based on contract renewal timing and pipeline strength, and flags accounts with risk exposure. These are insights that traditional models missed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Because there’s no coding barrier, every finance professional on the team becomes a data analyst. Teams customize agents for different regions or business units, and the insights refresh automatically.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="use-case-2-weekly-business-reviews-from-6-hours-to-10-minutes"&gt;Use case 2: Weekly business reviews from 6 hours to 10 minutes&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If target setting is a periodic deep dive, regular business reviews are the recurring ritual that occupies FP&amp;amp;A teams everywhere. At AWS, every week, insights on revenue performance need to be compiled, analyzed, and packaged for leadership. And every week, that preparation consumes an entire Monday.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The same AWS Finance team solved this by deploying Amazon Quick chat agents specific to each geographic region, connected through &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/flows/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Flows&lt;/a&gt; to automate workflows that run on a set cadence without manual intervention.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/artifacts/DBSBlogs/ML-20844/ML-20844-2.gif" alt="Blank Revenue Performance Analysis Flow that automates weekly business review workflows." width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Video showing a blank Revenue Performance Analysis Flow that helps automate weekly business review workflows.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Here’s what changed:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Before:&lt;/strong&gt; Every Monday, FP&amp;amp;A analysts spent a full morning compiling data from multiple systems, analyzing trends, manually reaching out to sales leads for customer anecdotes, and preparing talk tracks so leaders could understand what happened with revenue and why. The process was manual, repetitive, and left little time for strategic work.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;After:&lt;/strong&gt; Quick runs the Flow automatically each Monday morning. Region-specific chat agents analyze revenue performance across multiple dimensions: by charge type, by customer segment, and by growth contribution. They prepare comprehensive insights with ready-to-use talk tracks for leadership. Fresh analysis is waiting before the workday begins.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Quick doesn’t only report numbers. It connects structured data from financial systems with unstructured insights from field reports to get to the &lt;em&gt;why&lt;/em&gt; behind the trends. It examines customers across over a dozen dimensions, identifies patterns, and flags anomalies with context.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;blockquote&gt;
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;“These insights are prepared automatically every Monday morning. Our team now spends time on strategic priorities instead of compiling disparate data. We spend more time on the why and on driving business outcomes.”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/blockquote&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;— Geoff Winkler&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="the-pattern-from-data-compilation-to-strategic-partnership"&gt;The pattern: from data compilation to strategic partnership&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;These two use cases share a common thread. In both, the bottleneck wasn’t analytical skill, it was data compilation. Data was scattered across systems. Getting a complete picture required hours of manual extraction before any real analysis could begin.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Quick removes that bottleneck by connecting directly to enterprise data sources and letting finance professionals interact with their data through natural language. The result isn’t incremental efficiency. It changes how finance teams spend their time:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Workflow&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Before Amazon Quick&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;With Amazon Quick&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Target setting&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Approximately 6 hours per customer; one-third of portfolio covered&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Approximately 10 minutes per customer; entire portfolio covered with greater depth&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Weekly Business Review preparation&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Full Monday morning of manual compilation and analysis&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Automated weekly; insights ready before the workday begins&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Team focus&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Data compilation and query writing&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Strategic analysis and business partnership&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Across these use cases, the AWS Sales and Marketing Finance team reduced target-setting time from 6 hours to approximately 10 minutes per customer deep dive. They also removed the manual Monday routine for weekly business review preparation entirely. The time reclaimed went directly back into strategic work: risk analysis, customer anecdote synthesis, and identifying opportunities for growth.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="what-this-means-for-your-finance-team"&gt;What this means for your finance team&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You don’t need to face Amazon-scale complexity to benefit. Every finance team deals with fragmented data, recurring reporting cycles, and the tension between compiling numbers and actually using them.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon Quick is designed for business users. Finance professionals set up chat agents and automated workflows themselves, without engineering support. They customize agents for their specific needs, refine them through iteration, and expand them across the organization as results prove out.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If your team is spending more time preparing insights than delivering them, that’s the gap Quick is built to close.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Learn more about &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/finance" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Quick for Finance&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;In the next post in this series, we will explore how AWS Finance teams are using Quick to automate cost optimization and streamline approval workflows, turning hours of manual analysis into minutes.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;

		<item>
		<title>From Hugging Face to Amazon SageMaker Studio in one click</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/from-hugging-face-to-amazon-sagemaker-studio-in-one-click-2/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Hazim Qudah]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 06 Jul 2026 22:35:55 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker AI]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">36aa20434afd5b9e42aa8e0699f10369d99e0974</guid>

					<description>Today, we’re excited to announce a deep-link integration between Hugging Face and Amazon SageMaker AI. Developers can now go from model discovery to hands-on experimentation in SageMaker Studio with a single selection.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Today, we’re excited to announce a deep-link integration between &lt;a href="https://huggingface.co/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Hugging Face&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/ai/studio/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker AI&lt;/a&gt;. Developers can now go from model discovery to hands-on experimentation in SageMaker Studio with a single selection. Whether you fine-tune a foundation model (FM) from &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/ai/jumpstart/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker JumpStart&lt;/a&gt; or deploy it to an &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/ai/deploy/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker Inference&lt;/a&gt; endpoint, you can now land directly inside the relevant SageMaker Studio workflow. Your selected model is pre-loaded, and the environment is fully configured and ready to go.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Previously, getting started on SageMaker Studio after discovering a model on Hugging Face required navigating multiple steps. These included opening Amazon SageMaker AI on the AWS Management Console, creating a domain, configuring AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) permissions, and sometimes requesting graphics processing unit (GPU) quota. For developers who want to iterate quickly, this friction slows down the path from inspiration to experimentation. The integration creates a more direct path from discovery to enterprise deployment.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;blockquote&gt;
 &lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;“At Arcee, we build open models so developers and enterprises can actually own what they run: inspect the weights, post-train on their own data, and deploy on their own terms. This integration takes that promise the last mile. Going from an open model on Hugging Face straight into SageMaker Studio in a single click, then fine-tuning or deploying it inside your own AWS environment with nothing to wire up, is the kind of experience open models have been missing. Open weights you own, running in the cloud you control. That is exactly the combination our customers have been asking for.”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/blockquote&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;— Mark McQuade, Founder and CEO, Arcee AI&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With the launch of a one-click Studio landing experience, choosing &lt;em&gt;Customize on SageMaker AI&lt;/em&gt; or &lt;em&gt;Deploy on SageMaker AI&lt;/em&gt; on a supported Hugging Face model page takes you directly to the console. SageMaker AI then automatically provisions a new domain with pre-configured permissions in seconds and carries the model context through.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="whats-new"&gt;What’s new&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This launch introduces three capabilities that shorten the path from a Hugging Face model to a working SageMaker Studio workflow.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="deep-links-from-hugging-face-into-sagemaker-studio"&gt;Deep links from Hugging Face into SageMaker Studio&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When you browse models on Hugging Face, you’ll now see action buttons alongside supported models that map directly to SageMaker Studio workflows:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Customize on SageMaker AI&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;opens the Model Customization page in Studio with the selected model pre-loaded, ready to fine-tune.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Deploy on SageMaker AI&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;opens the Deployment page in Studio with the model pre-configured for endpoint deployment.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each entry point preserves the context, meaning you don’t need to search for the model again once inside Studio.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="pre-configured-permissions"&gt;Pre-configured permissions&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;New Studio environments created through this flow come with permissions already configured for the full range of SageMaker AI capabilities, including model customization, training jobs, notebook experimentation, and endpoint deployment. A new managed policy, &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/about-aws/whats-new/2026/01/quick-setup-model-customization-sagemaker-studio/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AmazonSageMakerModelCustomizationCoreAccess&lt;/a&gt;, is created and attached for you. It provides permissions for serverless model customization jobs using supervised fine-tuning (SFT), direct preference optimization (DPO), reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR), and reinforcement learning from AI feedback (RLAIF), with supported deployment to SageMaker AI or Amazon Bedrock endpoints. This alleviates the need to manually create and configure AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) roles and policies before you can start experimenting. For existing Studio environments, actionable messages with direct links to documentation guide you through adding these permissions.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="gpu-quota-visibility"&gt;GPU quota visibility&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When selecting instance types for deployment or training, the Studio UI now surfaces quota availability directly in the instance selection list. You can immediately see which GPU instance types (G5, G6) are available under your account’s current limits. You don’t need to navigate separately to Service Quotas. If you still need to request a limit increase, you’re redirected directly to the Service Quotas page for the respective instance type.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="walkthrough-deep-linking-from-hugging-face-to-sagemaker-studio"&gt;Walkthrough: Deep-linking from Hugging Face to SageMaker Studio&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Let’s walk through the experience of customizing or deploying a model starting from Hugging Face.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-1-discover-and-select"&gt;Step 1: Discover and select&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;On the Hugging Face model page, select &lt;strong&gt;Customize on SageMaker AI&lt;/strong&gt; for a supported model.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/06/ML-21254-1.png" alt="Hugging Face model page showing the Customize on SageMaker AI button for a supported model" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-2-sign-in"&gt;Step 2: Sign in&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You’re prompted to sign in to AWS using your existing credentials. If you already have an active console session, this step is skipped automatically. For more information, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/signin/latest/userguide/how-to-sign-in.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Sign in to the AWS Management Console&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-3-land-in-studio"&gt;Step 3: Land in Studio&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You arrive directly on the Model Customization page inside SageMaker Studio with your model pre-selected. Next, configure your fine-tuning parameters such as training data, hyperparameters, and instance type, then submit the customization job.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/06/ML-21254-2.png" alt="SageMaker Studio Model Customization page with the selected model pre-loaded and fine-tuning parameters ready to configure" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Alternatively, selecting &lt;strong&gt;Deploy on SageMaker AI&lt;/strong&gt; opens the endpoint deployment page in Studio with the model pre-configured. Select your instance type (quota visibility included), review the settings, and deploy.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/06/ML-21254-3.png" alt="SageMaker Studio endpoint deployment page with the model pre-configured and instance type selection showing quota visibility" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="step-4-test-your-endpoint"&gt;Step 4: Test your endpoint&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After you deploy your endpoint, test inference directly from Studio’s endpoint &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/manage-endpoints-studio-test.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;testing interface&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="getting-started"&gt;Getting started&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can try this experience today:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Browse models on Hugging Face.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Look for the &lt;strong&gt;Customize on SageMaker AI&lt;/strong&gt; or &lt;strong&gt;Deploy on SageMaker AI&lt;/strong&gt; buttons on supported models.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Select and follow the streamlined sign-in flow.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Start building in a fully configured SageMaker Studio environment.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The launch of a one-click Studio landing experience minimizes the friction between discovering a model and experimenting with it. By connecting Hugging Face directly to the SageMaker Studio workflows, developers can stay in their flow. There’s no context switching, no manual environment setup, and no permission troubleshooting.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To get started, visit the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/ai/studio/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker Studio&lt;/a&gt; page or explore models on Hugging Face and choose Deploy or Customize on SageMaker AI.&lt;/p&gt;
